The style was characterised by long, convoluted sentences and a predilection for rare words and neologisms.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The "\xc3\x86thelstan A" charters were written in <i>hermeneutic</i> Latin. In the view of Simon Keynes it is no coincidence that they first appear immediately after the king had for the first time united England under his rule, and they show a high level of intellectual attainment and a monarchy invigorated by success and adopting the trappings of a new political order.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The style influenced architects of the late tenth-century monastic reformers educated at \xc3\x86thelstan\'s court such as \xc3\x86thelwold and Dunstan, and became a hallmark of the movement.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> After "\xc3\x86thelstan A", charters became more simple, but the <i>hermeneutic</i> style returned in the charters of Eadwig and Edgar.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The historian <a href="/wiki/W._H._Stevenson" title="W. H. Stevenson">W. H. Stevenson</a> commented in 1898:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>The object of the compilers of these charters was to express their meaning by the use of the greatest possible number of words and by the choice of the most grandiloquent, bombastic words they could find. Every sentence is so overloaded by the heaping up of unnecessary words that the meaning is almost buried out of sight. The invocation with its appended clauses, opening with pompous and partly alliterative words, will proceed amongst a blaze of verbal fireworks throughout twenty lines of smallish type, and the pyrotechnic display will be maintained with equal magnificence throughout the whole charter, leaving the reader, dazzled by the glaze and blinded by the smoke, in a state of uncertainty as to the meaning of these frequently untranslatable and usually interminable sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>However, Michael Lapidge argues that however unpalatable the <i>hermeneutic</i> style seems to modern taste, it was an important part of late Anglo-Saxon culture, and deserves more sympathetic attention than it has received from modern historians.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In the view of historian David Woodman, "\xc3\x86thelstan A" should "be accorded recognition as an individual author of no little genius, a man who not only overhauled the legal form of the diploma but also had the ability to write Latin that is as enduringly fascinating as it is complex&#160;... In many ways the diplomas of "\xc3\x86thelstan A" represent the stylistic peak of the Anglo-Saxon diplomatic tradition, a fitting complement to \xc3\x86thelstan\'s own momentous political feats and to the forging of what would become England."<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_monarch">British monarch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: British monarch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="\xc3\x86thelstan in a fifteenth-century stained glass window" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg/220px-Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg/330px-Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg/440px-Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2572" data-file-height="3638" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\xc3\x86thelstan in a fifteenth-century stained glass window in <a href="/wiki/All_Souls_College,_Oxford" title="All Souls College, Oxford">All Souls College Chapel, Oxford</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Historians frequently comment on \xc3\x86thelstan\'s grand and extravagant titles. On his coins and charters he is described as <i>Rex totius Britanniae</i>, or "King of the whole of Britain". A gospel book he donated to <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Canterbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ Church, Canterbury">Christ Church, Canterbury</a> is inscribed "\xc3\x86thelstan, king of the English and ruler of the whole of Britain with a devout mind gave this book to the primatial see of Canterbury, to the church dedicated to Christ". In charters from 931 he is "king of the English, elevated by the right hand of the almighty to the throne of the whole kingdom of Britain", and in one manuscript dedication he is even styled "<a href="/wiki/Basileus" title="Basileus">basileus</a> et curagulus", the titles of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine emperors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Some historians are not impressed. "Clearly", comments Alex Woolf, "King \xc3\x86thelstan was a man who had pretensions," <sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> while in the view of Simon Keynes, "\xc3\x86thelstan A" proclaimed his master king of Britain "by wishful extension".<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes61_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes61-131">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> But according to George Molyneaux "this is to apply an anachronistic standard: tenth-century kings had a loose but real hegemony throughout the island, and their titles only appear inflated if one assumes that kingship ought to involve domination of an intensity like that seen within the English kingdom of the eleventh and later centuries."<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Foreign contemporaries described him in panegyrical terms. The French chronicler <a href="/wiki/Flodoard" title="Flodoard">Flodoard</a> described him as "the king from overseas", and the <i>Annals of Ulster</i> as the "pillar of the dignity of the western world".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Some historians take a similar view. Michael Wood titled an essay, "The Making of King Aethelstan\'s Empire: an English Charlemagne?", and described him as "the most powerful ruler that Britain had seen since the Romans".<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In the view of Veronica Ortenberg, he was "the most powerful ruler in Europe" with an army that had repeatedly defeated the Vikings; continental rulers saw him as a Carolingian emperor, who "was clearly treated as the new Charlemagne". She wrote:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Wessex kings carried an aura of power and success, which made them increasingly powerful in the 920s, while most Continental houses were in military trouble and engaged in internecine warfare. While the civil wars and the Viking attacks on the Continent had spelled the end of unity of the Carolingian empire, which had already disintegrated into separate kingdoms, military success had enabled \xc3\x86thelstan to triumph at home and to attempt to go beyond the reputation of a great heroic dynasty of warrior kings, in order to develop a Carolingian ideology of kingship.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_relations">European relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: European relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The West Saxon court had connections with the <a href="/wiki/Carolingians" class="mw-redirect" title="Carolingians">Carolingians</a> going back to the marriage between \xc3\x86thelstan\'s great-grandfather <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelwulf_of_Wessex" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x86thelwulf of Wessex">\xc3\x86thelwulf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judith_of_Flanders" title="Judith of Flanders">Judith</a>, daughter of the king of West Francia (and future Holy Roman Emperor), <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bald" title="Charles the Bald">Charles the Bald</a>, as well as the marriage of Alfred the Great\'s daughter, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfthryth,_Countess_of_Flanders" title="\xc3\x86lfthryth, Countess of Flanders">\xc3\x86lfthryth</a> to Judith\'s son by a later marriage, <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_II,_Count_of_Flanders" class="mw-redirect" title="Baldwin II, Count of Flanders">Baldwin II, Count of Flanders</a>. One of \xc3\x86thelstan\'s half-sisters, <a href="/wiki/Eadgifu_of_Wessex" title="Eadgifu of Wessex">Eadgifu</a>, married <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Simple" title="Charles the Simple">Charles the Simple</a>, king of the <a href="/wiki/West_Franks" class="mw-redirect" title="West Franks">West Franks</a>, in the late 910s. He was deposed in 922, and Eadgifu sent their son, <a href="/wiki/Louis_IV_of_France" title="Louis IV of France">Louis</a> to safety in England. By \xc3\x86thelstan\'s time the connection was well established, and his coronation was performed with the Carolingian ceremony of anointment, probably to draw a deliberate parallel between his rule and Carolingian tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> His "crowned bust" coinage of 933\xe2\x80\x93938 was the first Anglo-Saxon coinage to show the king crowned, following Carolingian iconography.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Like his father, \xc3\x86thelstan was unwilling to marry his female relatives to his own subjects, so his sisters either entered nunneries or married foreign husbands. This was one reason for his close relations with European courts, and he married several of his half-sisters to European nobles<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> in what historian Sheila Sharp called "a flurry of dynastic bridal activity unequalled again until Queen Victoria\'s time".<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Another reason lay in the common interest on both sides of the Channel in resisting the threat from the Vikings, while the rise in the power and reputation of the royal house of Wessex made marriage with an English princess more prestigious to European rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> In 926 <a href="/wiki/Hugh_the_Great" title="Hugh the Great">Hugh</a>, Duke of the Franks, sent \xc3\x86thelstan\'s cousin, Adelolf, Count of Boulogne, on an embassy to ask for the hand of one of \xc3\x86thelstan\'s sisters. According to William of Malmesbury, the gifts Adelolf brought included spices, jewels, many swift horses, a crown of solid gold, the sword of <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a>, Charlemagne\'s lance, and a piece of the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Thorns" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown of Thorns">Crown of Thorns</a>. \xc3\x86thelstan sent his half-sister <a href="/wiki/Eadhild" title="Eadhild">Eadhild</a> to be Hugh\'s wife.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\xc3\x86thelstan\'s most important European alliance was with the new <a href="/wiki/Liudolfing" class="mw-redirect" title="Liudolfing">Liudolfing</a> dynasty in <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a>. The Carolingian dynasty of East Francia had died out in the early tenth century, and its new Liudolfing king, <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Fowler" title="Henry the Fowler">Henry the Fowler</a>, was seen by many as an <i>arriviste</i>. He needed a royal marriage for his son to establish his legitimacy, but no suitable Carolingian princesses were available. The ancient royal line of the West Saxons provided an acceptable alternative, especially as they (wrongly) claimed descent from the seventh-century king and saint, <a href="/wiki/Oswald_of_Northumbria" title="Oswald of Northumbria">Oswald</a>, who was venerated in Germany. In 929 or 930 Henry sent ambassadors to \xc3\x86thelstan\'s court seeking a wife for his son, Otto, who later became Holy Roman Emperor.  \xc3\x86thelstan sent two of his half-sisters, and Otto chose <a href="/wiki/Eadgyth" title="Eadgyth">Eadgyth</a>. Fifty years later, \xc3\x86thelweard, a descendant of Alfred the Great\'s older brother, addressed his Latin version of the <i>Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</i> to <a href="/wiki/Mathilde,_Abbess_of_Essen" title="Mathilde, Abbess of Essen">Mathilde, Abbess of Essen</a>, who was Eadgyth\'s granddaughter, and had apparently requested it. The other sister, whose name is uncertain, was married to a prince from near the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> who has not definitely been identified.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early medieval Europe, it was common for kings to act as foster-fathers for the sons of other kings. \xc3\x86thelstan was known for the support he gave to dispossessed young royalty. In 936 he sent an English fleet to help his foster-son, <a href="/wiki/Alan_II,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="Alan II, Duke of Brittany">Alan II, Duke of Brittany</a>, to regain his ancestral lands, which had been conquered by the Vikings.  In the same year he assisted the son of his half-sister Eadgifu, Louis, to take the throne of West Francia, and in 939 he sent another fleet that unsuccessfully attempted to help Louis in a struggle with rebellious magnates. According to later Scandinavian sources, he helped another possible foster-son, <a href="/wiki/Haakon_the_Good" title="Haakon the Good">Hakon</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Harald_Fairhair" title="Harald Fairhair">Harald Fairhair</a>, king of <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, to reclaim his throne,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> and he was known among Norwegians as "\xc3\x86thelstan the Good".<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\xc3\x86thelstan\'s court was perhaps the most cosmopolitan of the Anglo-Saxon period.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The close contacts between the English and European courts ended soon after his death, but descent from the English royal house long remained a source of prestige for continental ruling families.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> According to Frank Stenton in his history of the period, <i>Anglo-Saxon England</i>, "Between Offa and <a href="/wiki/Cnut_the_Great" title="Cnut the Great">Cnut</a> there is no English king who played so prominent or so sustained a part in the general affairs of Europe."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg/260px-King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg" width="260" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg/390px-King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg/520px-King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1065" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Empty fifteenth-century tomb of King \xc3\x86thelstan at <a href="/wiki/Malmesbury_Abbey" title="Malmesbury Abbey">Malmesbury Abbey</a></div></div></div>\n<p>\xc3\x86thelstan died at <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a> on 27 October 939. His grandfather Alfred, his father Edward, and his half-brother \xc3\x86lfweard had been buried at Winchester, but \xc3\x86thelstan chose not to honour the city associated with opposition to his rule. By his own wish he was buried at <a href="/wiki/Malmesbury_Abbey" title="Malmesbury Abbey">Malmesbury Abbey</a>, where he had buried his cousins who died at Brunanburh. No other member of the West Saxon royal family was buried there, and according to William of Malmesbury, \xc3\x86thelstan\'s choice reflected his devotion to the abbey and to the memory of its seventh-century abbot, Saint Aldhelm. William described \xc3\x86thelstan as fair-haired "as I have seen for myself in his remains, beautifully intertwined with gold threads". His bones were lost during the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">Reformation</a>, but he is commemorated by an empty fifteenth-century tomb.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>After \xc3\x86thelstan\'s death, the men of York immediately chose the Viking king of Dublin, Olaf Guthfrithson (or his cousin, <a href="/wiki/Anlaf_Cuaran" class="mw-redirect" title="Anlaf Cuaran">Anlaf Cuaran</a><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;n&#93;</a></sup>), as their king, and Anglo-Saxon control of the north, seemingly made safe by the victory of Brunanburh, collapsed. The reigns of \xc3\x86thelstan\'s half-brothers <a href="/wiki/Edmund_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund I of England">Edmund</a> (939\xe2\x80\x93946) and Eadred (946\xe2\x80\x93955) were largely devoted to regaining control. Olaf seized the east midlands, leading to the establishment of a frontier at <a href="/wiki/Watling_Street" title="Watling Street">Watling Street</a>. In 941 Olaf died, and Edmund took back control of the east midlands, and then York in 944. Following Edmund\'s death York again switched back to Viking control, and it was only when the Northumbrians finally drove out their Norwegian Viking king <a href="/wiki/Eric_Bloodaxe" title="Eric Bloodaxe">Eric Bloodaxe</a> in 954 and submitted to Eadred that Anglo-Saxon control of the whole of England was finally restored.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_sources">Primary sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Primary sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">Chronicle</a> sources for the life of \xc3\x86thelstan are limited, and the first biography, by Sarah Foot, was only published in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i> in \xc3\x86thelstan\'s reign is principally devoted to military events, and it is largely silent apart from recording his most important victories.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumville_167_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumville_167-153">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> An important source is the twelfth-century chronicle of William of Malmesbury, but historians are cautious about accepting his testimony, much of which cannot be verified from other sources. David Dumville goes so far as to dismiss William\'s account entirely, regarding him as a "treacherous witness" whose account is unfortunately influential.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> However, Sarah Foot is inclined to accept Michael Wood\'s argument that William\'s chronicle draws on a lost life of \xc3\x86thelstan. She cautions, however, that we have no means of discovering how far William "improved" on the original.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Dumville\'s view, \xc3\x86thelstan has been regarded by historians as a shadowy figure because of an ostensible lack of source material, but he argues that the lack is more apparent than real.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Charters, law codes, and coins throw considerable light on \xc3\x86thelstan\'s government.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The scribe known to historians as "\xc3\x86thelstan A", who was responsible for drafting all charters between 928 and 935, provides very detailed information, including signatories, dates, and locations, illuminating \xc3\x86thelstan\'s progress around his realm. "\xc3\x86thelstan A" may have been Bishop <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfwine_of_Lichfield" title="\xc3\x86lfwine of Lichfield">\xc3\x86lfwine of Lichfield</a>, who was close to the king.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> By contrast with this extensive source of information, no charters survive from 910 to 924, a gap which historians struggle to explain, and which makes it difficult to assess the degree of continuity in personnel and the operation of government between the reigns of Edward and \xc3\x86thelstan.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Historians are also paying increasing attention to less conventional sources, such as contemporary poetry in his praise and manuscripts associated with his name.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The reign of \xc3\x86thelstan has been overshadowed by the achievements of his grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a>, but he is now considered one of the greatest kings of the West Saxon dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-DarkAge_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DarkAge-161">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Modern historians endorse the view of twelfth century chronicler William of Malmesbury that "no one more just or more learned ever governed the kingdom".<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Frank Stenton and Simon Keynes both describe him as the one Anglo-Saxon king who will bear comparison with Alfred. In Keynes\'s view he "has long been regarded, with good reason, as a towering figure in the landscape of the tenth century&#160;... he has also been hailed as the first king of England, as a statesman of international standing".<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> David Dumville describes \xc3\x86thelstan as "the father of mediaeval and modern England",<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> while Michael Wood regards <a href="/wiki/Offa_of_Mercia" title="Offa of Mercia">Offa</a>, Alfred, and \xc3\x86thelstan as the three greatest Anglo-Saxon kings, and \xc3\x86thelstan as "one of the more important lay intellectuals in Anglo-Saxon history".<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\xc3\x86thelstan is regarded as the first King of England by some modern historians.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;o&#93;</a></sup> Although it was Eadred who would achieve the final unification of England by the permanent conquest of Viking York, \xc3\x86thelstan\'s campaigns made this success possible.<sup id="cite_ref-DarkAge_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DarkAge-161">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> His nephew Edgar called himself King of the English and revived the claim to rule over all the peoples of Britain. Simon Keynes argued that "the consistent usages of Edgar\'s reign represent nothing less than a determined reaffirmation of the polity created by \xc3\x86thelstan in the 930s".<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Historian Charles Insley, however, sees \xc3\x86thelstan\'s hegemony as fragile: "The level of overlordship wielded by \xc3\x86thelstan during the 930s over the rest of Britain was perhaps not attained again by an English king until <a href="/wiki/Edward_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward I">Edward I</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> George Molyneaux argues that:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>The tendency of some modern historians to celebrate \xc3\x86thelstan as "the first king of England" is, however, problematic, since there is little sign that that in his day the title <i>rex Anglorum</i> was closely or consistently tied to an area similar to that which we consider England. Indeed, when \xc3\x86thelstan\'s rule was associated with any definite geographical expanse, the territory in question was usually the whole island of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Simon Keynes saw \xc3\x86thelstan\'s law-making as his greatest achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-Keynes_1999_471_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes_1999_471-90">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> His reign predates the sophisticated state of the later Anglo-Saxon period, but his creation of the most centralised government England had yet seen, with the king and his council working strategically to ensure acceptance of his authority and laws, laid the foundations on which his brothers and nephews would create one of the wealthiest and most advanced systems of government in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> \xc3\x86thelstan\'s reign built upon his grandfather\'s ecclesiastical programme, consolidating the local ecclesiastical revival and laying the foundation for the monastic reform movement later in the century.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumville_167_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumville_167-153">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\xc3\x86thelstan\'s reputation was at its height when he died. According to Sarah Foot, "He found acclaim in his own day not only as a successful military leader and effective monarch but also as a man of devotion, committed to the promotion of religion and the patronage of learning." Later in the century, \xc3\x86thelweard praised him as a very mighty king worthy of honour, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred_the_Unready" title="\xc3\x86thelred the Unready">\xc3\x86thelred the Unready</a>, who named his eight sons after his predecessors, put \xc3\x86thelstan first as the name of his eldest son.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In his biography of \xc3\x86thelred, Levi Roach commented:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>The king was clearly proud of his family and the fact that \xc3\x86thelstan stands atop this list speaks volumes: though later overtaken by Alfred the Great in fame, in the 980s it must have seemed as if everything had begun with the king\'s great-uncle (a view with which many modern historians would be inclined to concur).<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Memory of \xc3\x86thelstan then declined until it was revived by William of Malmesbury, who took a special interest in him as the one king who had chosen to be buried in his own house. William\'s account kept his memory alive, and he was praised by other medieval chroniclers. In the early sixteenth century <a href="/wiki/William_Tyndale" title="William Tyndale">William Tyndale</a> justified his English translation of the Bible by stating that he had read that King \xc3\x86thelstan had caused the Holy Scriptures to be translated into Anglo-Saxon.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From the sixteenth century onwards Alfred\'s reputation became dominant and \xc3\x86thelstan largely disappeared from popular consciousness. <a href="/wiki/Sharon_Turner" title="Sharon Turner">Sharon Turner</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Anglo-Saxons" title="History of the Anglo-Saxons">History of the Anglo-Saxons</a></i>, first published between 1799 and 1805, played a crucial role in promoting Anglo-Saxon studies, and he helped to establish Brunanburh as a key battle in English history, but his treatment of \xc3\x86thelstan was slight in comparison with Alfred. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> had only one paragraph on \xc3\x86thelstan in his <i><a href="/wiki/A_Child%27s_History_of_England" title="A Child&#39;s History of England">Child\'s History of England</a></i>, and although Anglo-Saxon history was a popular subject for nineteenth-century artists, and Alfred was frequently depicted in paintings at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> between 1769 and 1904, there was not one picture of \xc3\x86thelstan.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to Michael Wood: "Among all the great rulers of British history, \xc3\x86thelstan today is the forgotten man",<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-176">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> and in medieval historian <a href="/wiki/Ann_Williams_(historian)" title="Ann Williams (historian)">Ann Williams</a>\'s view: "If \xc3\x86thelstan has not had the reputation which accrued to his grandfather, the fault lies in the surviving sources; \xc3\x86thelstan had no biographer, and the <i>Chronicle</i> for his reign is scanty. In his own day he was \'the roof-tree of the honour of the western world\'".<sup id="cite_ref-DarkAge_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DarkAge-161">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r865274394">.mw-parser-output table.ahnentafel{border-collapse:separate;border-spacing:0;line-height:130%}.mw-parser-output .ahnentafel tr{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ahnentafel-t{border-top:#000 solid 1px;border-left:#000 solid 1px}.mw-parser-output .ahnentafel-b{border-bottom:#000 solid 1px;border-left:#000 solid 1px}</style><div class="noresize"><table class="collapsible collapsed" style="margin:0.3em auto;clear:none;min-width:60em;width:auto;font-size:88%;border:1px solid #aaa"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.3em 0.2em 4.3em;background:none;width:auto">Ancestors of \xc3\x86thelstan<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup></th></tr><tr><td style="text-align:center"><table class="ahnentafel" style="margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><tbody><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="60" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="29"> </td><td rowspan="28" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="13"> </td><td rowspan="12" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="5"> </td><td rowspan="4" colspan="2"> </td><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #fcc;">1. <b>\xc3\x86thelstan</b></td><td rowspan="2" colspan="15"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="32" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="31" class="ahnentafel-b"> </td><td rowspan="30" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="15"> </td><td rowspan="14" </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="7"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="6" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="7"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="15"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="14" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="7"> </td><td rowspan="6" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="7"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="6" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #fb9;">3. <a href="/wiki/Ecgwynn" title="Ecgwynn">Ecgwynn</a></td><td rowspan="2" colspan="11"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pronounced <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Old_English" title="Help:IPA/Old English">[\xcb\x88\xc3\xa6\xc3\xb0e\xc9\xabst\xc9\x91n]</a></span>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pronounced <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Old_English" title="Help:IPA/Old English">[\xcb\x88\xc3\xa6\xc3\xb0e\xc9\xabst\xc9\x91\xcb\x90n]</a></span>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">9th-century West Saxon kings before Alfred the Great are generally described by historians as kings of Wessex or of the West Saxons. In the 880s \xc3\x86thelred, Lord of the Mercians, accepted West Saxon lordship, and Alfred then adopted a new title, king of the Anglo-Saxons, representing his conception of a new polity of all the English people who were not under Viking rule. This endured until 927, when \xc3\x86thelstan conquered Viking York, and adopted the title <i>rex anglorum</i> (king of the English), in recognition of his rule over the whole of England. The term "Englalonde" (England) came into use in the late 10th or early 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An allusion in the twelfth-century <a href="/wiki/Liber_Eliensis" title="Liber Eliensis">Liber Eliensis</a> to "Eadgyth, daughter of king \xc3\x86thelstan" is probably a mistaken reference to his sister.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An exception is George Molyneaux, who states that "There are, however, grounds to suspect that \xc3\x86thelstan may have had a hand in the death of \xc3\x86lfweard\'s full brother Edwin in 933".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Historians generally describe her as his only full sister, but Maggie Bailey points out that this rests on the late testimony of William of Malmesbury, and the Anglo-Saxon Chronicle makes no such distinction when recording her marriage to Sihtric. William did not know her name, but traditions first recorded at <a href="/wiki/Bury,_Greater_Manchester" title="Bury, Greater Manchester">Bury</a> in the early twelfth century identify her as <a href="/wiki/Edith_of_Polesworth" title="Edith of Polesworth">Saint Edith of Polesworth</a>. This is considered uncertain, but it is likely that she entered a nunnery in widowhood.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some historians believe that Sihtric renounced his wife soon after the marriage and reverted to paganism,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> while others merely state that \xc3\x86thelstan took advantage of Sihtric\'s death to invade.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In the view of Alex Woolf, it is unlikely that Sihtric repudiated her because \xc3\x86thelstan would almost certainly have declared war on him.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to William of Malmesbury it was Owain of Strathclyde who was present at Eamont, but the Anglo-Saxon Chronicle says Owain of Gwent. It could have been both.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">William of Malmesbury\'s report of the Hereford meeting is not mentioned in the first volume of the Oxford History of Wales, <i>Wales and the Britons 350\xe2\x80\x931064</i> by Thomas Charles-Edwards.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The situation in northern Northumbria, however, is unclear. In the view of Ann Williams, the submission of Ealdred of Bamburgh was probably nominal, and it is likely that he acknowledged Constantine as his lord, but Alex Woolf sees Ealdred as a semi-independent ruler acknowledging West Saxon authority, like \xc3\x86thelred of Mercia a generation earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the view of Janet Nelson, \xc3\x86thelstan had limited control over the north-west, and the donation of Amounderness in an area which had recently attracted many Scandinavian immigrants to "a powerful, but far from reliable, local potentate" was "a political gesture rather than a sign of prior control."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>The Battle of Brunanburh: A Casebook</i> includes two versions of the poem and translations by Michael Livingston and Robert P. Creed, and Alex Woolf gives his own translation in <i>From Pictland to Alba</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Wormald discusses the codes in detail in <i>The Making of English Law</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Historian Kevin Halloran argues that it was Anlaf Cuaran rather than Olaf Guthfrithson who became King of York after \xc3\x86thelstan\'s death<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-167"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-167">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">David Dumville\'s chapter on \xc3\x86thelstan in <i>Wessex and England</i> is headed \'Between Alfred the Great and Edgar the Peacemaker: \xc3\x86thelstan, The First King of England\', and the title of Sarah Foot\'s biography is <i>\xc3\x86thelstan: The First King of England</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em;
